title="Epictetus">Epictetus</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Gerolamo_Cardano" title="Gerolamo Cardano">Gerolamo Cardano</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCS_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-MCS-2">[2]</a></sup></li></ul></div></div></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr class="note"><td colspan="2" style="text-align:center"><div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border: none; margin-right:auto;"><div class="hlist hlist-separated"><ul><li><a href="/wiki/William_Lane_Craig" title="William Lane Craig">William Lane Craig</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Antoine_Arnauld" title="Antoine Arnauld">Antoine Arnauld</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Pierre_Duhem" title="Pierre Duhem">Pierre Duhem</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/William_James" title="William James">William James</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">G. W. Leibniz</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Brunschvicg" title="L&#233;on Brunschvicg">L&#233;on Brunschvicg</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Alexis_de_Tocqueville" title="Alexis de Tocqueville">Alexis de Tocqueville</a></li><li><a href="/wiki/Henri_Bergson" title="Henri Bergson">Henri Bergson</a></li></ul></div></div></li></ul>\n</div></td></tr><tr style="display:none"><td colspan="2">\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Blaise Pascal</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="\'p\' in \'pie\'">p</span><span title="/&#230;/: \'a\' in \'bad\'">&#230;</span><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/&#230;/: \'a\' in \'bad\'">&#230;</span><span title="\'l\' in \'lie\'">l</span></span>, <wbr/><span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="\'p\' in \'pie\'">p</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;/: \'a\' in \'father\'">&#593;&#720;</span><span title="/&#712;/: primary stress follows">&#712;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span><span title="\'k\' in \'kind\'">k</span><span title="/&#593;&#720;/: \'a\' in \'father\'">&#593;&#720;</span><span title="\'l\' in \'lie\'">l</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> <small>French:&#160;</small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/French" title="Help:IPA/French">[bl&#603;z paskal]</a></span>; 19 June 1623 &#8211; 19 August 1662) was a French <a href="/wiki/Mathematician" title="Mathematician">mathematician</a>, <a href="/wiki/Physicist" title="Physicist">physicist</a>, inventor, writer and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> theologian. He was a <a href="/wiki/Child_prodigy" title="Child prodigy">child prodigy</a> who was educated by his father, a tax collector in <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>. Pascal\'s earliest work was in the natural and applied sciences where he made important contributions to the study of <a href="/wiki/Fluid" title="Fluid">fluids</a>, and clarified the concepts of <a href="/wiki/Pressure" title="Pressure">pressure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a> by generalising the work of <a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">Evangelista Torricelli</a>. Pascal also wrote in defence of the <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">scientific method</a>.\n</p><p>In 1642, while still a teenager, he started some pioneering work on calculating machines. After three years of effort and 50 prototypes,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> he built 20 finished machines (called <a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_calculator" title="Pascal\'s calculator">Pascal\'s calculators</a> and later Pascalines) over the following 10 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> establishing him as one of the first two inventors of the <a href="/wiki/Mechanical_calculator" title="Mechanical calculator">mechanical calculator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pascal was an important mathematician, helping create two major new areas of research: he wrote a significant treatise on the subject of <a href="/wiki/Projective_geometry" title="Projective geometry">projective geometry</a> at the age of 16, and later corresponded with <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a> on <a href="/wiki/Probability_theory" title="Probability theory">probability theory</a>, strongly influencing the development of modern economics and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Social_sciences" title="Social sciences">social science</a>. Following <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">Galileo Galilei</a> and Torricelli, in 1647, he rebutted <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>\'s followers who insisted that <a href="/wiki/Horror_vacui_(physics)" title="Horror vacui (physics)">nature abhors a vacuum</a>. Pascal\'s results caused many disputes before being accepted.\n</p><p>In 1646, he and his sister Jacqueline identified with the religious movement within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Catholicism" title="Catholicism">Catholicism</a> known by its detractors as <a href="/wiki/Jansenism" title="Jansenism">Jansenism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> Following a religious experience in late 1654, he began writing influential works on philosophy and theology. His two most famous works date from this period: the <i><a href="/wiki/Lettres_provinciales" title="Lettres provinciales">Lettres provinciales</a></i> and the <i><a href="/wiki/Pens%C3%A9es" title="Pens&#233;es">Pens&#233;es</a></i>, the former set in the conflict between Jansenists and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a>. In that year, he also wrote an important treatise on the arithmetical triangle. Between 1658 and 1659, he wrote on the <a href="/wiki/Cycloid" title="Cycloid">cycloid</a> and its use in calculating the volume of solids.\n</p><p>Throughout his life, Pascal was in frail health, especially after the age of 18; he died just two months after his 39th birthday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input href="#Last_works_and_death"><span class="tocnumber">4.5</span> <span class="toctext">Last works and death</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-11"><a href="#Legacy"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Legacy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-12"><a href="#Works"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Works</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-15"><a href="#Further_reading"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Further reading</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-16"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life_and_education">Early life and education</span></h2>\n<table class="box-Refimprove_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Refimprove" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>needs additional citations for <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">verification</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Blaise_Pascal&amp;action=edit">improve this article</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.<br/><small><span class="plainlinks"><i>Find sources:</i>&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?as_eq=wikipedia&amp;q=%22Blaise+Pascal%22" rel="nofollow">"Blaise Pascal"</a>&#160;&#8211;&#160;<a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbm=nws&amp;q=%22Blaise+Pascal%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">news</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?&amp;q=%22Blaise+Pascal%22+site:news.google.com/newspapers&amp;source=newspapers" rel="nofollow">newspapers</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//www.google.com/search?tbs=bks:1&amp;q=%22Blaise+Pascal%22+-wikipedia" rel="nofollow">books</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="//scholar.google.com/scholar?q=%22Blaise+Pascal%22" rel="nofollow">scholar</a>&#160;<b>&#183;</b> <a class="external text" href="https://www.jstor.org/action/doBasicSearch?Query=%22Blaise+Pascal%22&amp;acc=on&amp;wc=on" rel="nofollow">JSTOR</a></span></small></span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">June 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Pascal was born in <a href="/wiki/Clermont-Ferrand" title="Clermont-Ferrand">Clermont-Ferrand</a>, which is in France\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Auvergne_(region)" title="Auvergne (region)">Auvergne region</a>. He lost his mother, Antoinette Begon, at the age of three.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDevlin20_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDevlin20-10">[10]</a></sup> His father, <a href="/wiki/%C3%89tienne_Pascal" title="&#201;tienne Pascal">&#201;tienne Pascal</a> (1588&#8211;1651), who also had an interest in science and mathematics, was a local judge and member of the "<a href="/wiki/Nobles_of_the_Robe" title="Nobles of the Robe">Noblesse de Robe</a>". Pascal had two sisters, the younger <a href="/wiki/Jacqueline_Pascal" title="Jacqueline Pascal">Jacqueline</a> and the elder Gilberte.\n</p><p>In 1631, five years after the death of his wife,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MCS_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-MCS-2">[2]</a></sup> &#201;tienne Pascal moved with his children to Paris. The newly arrived family soon hired Louise Delfault, a maid who eventually became an instrumental member of the family. &#201;tienne, who never remarried, decided that he alone would educate his children, for they all showed extraordinary intellectual ability, particularly his son Blaise. The young Pascal showed an amazing aptitude for mathematics and science.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Blaise_Pascal_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1126" data-file-width="965" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Blaise_Pascal_2.jpg/220px-Blaise_Pascal_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Blaise_Pascal_2.jpg/330px-Blaise_Pascal_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Blaise_Pascal_2.jpg/440px-Blaise_Pascal_2.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Blaise_Pascal_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait of Pascal</div></div></div>\n<p>Particularly of interest to Pascal was a work of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/G%C3%A9rard_Desargues" title="G&#233;rard Desargues">Desargues</a> on <a href="/wiki/Conic_section" title="Conic section">conic sections</a>. Following Desargues\' thinking, the 16-year-old Pascal produced, as a means of proof, a short treatise on what was called the "Mystic Hexagram", <i>Essai pour les coniques</i> ("Essay on Conics") and sent it&#8212;his first serious work of mathematics&#8212;to <a href="/wiki/Marin_Mersenne" title="Marin Mersenne">P&#232;re Mersenne</a> in Paris; it is known still today as <a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_theorem" title="Pascal\'s theorem">Pascal\'s theorem</a>. It states that if a hexagon is inscribed in a circle (or conic) then the three intersection points of opposite sides lie on a line (called the Pascal line).\n</p><p>Pascal\'s work was so precocious that Descartes was convinced that Pascal\'s father had written it. When assured by Mersenne that it was, indeed, the product of the son and not the father, Descartes dismissed it with a sniff: "I do not find it strange that he has offered demonstrations about conics more appropriate than those of the ancients," adding, "but other matters related to this subject can be proposed that would scarcely occur to a 16-year-old child."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In France at that time offices and positions could be&#8212;and were&#8212;bought and sold. In 1631, &#201;tienne sold his position as second president of the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cour_des_Aides" title="Cour des Aides">Cour des Aides</a></i> for 65,665 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Livres" title="Livres">livres</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The money was invested in a <a href="/wiki/Government_bond" title="Government bond">government bond</a> which provided, if not a lavish, then certainly a comfortable income which allowed the Pascal family to move to, and enjoy, Paris. But in 1638 Richelieu, desperate for money to carry on the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Years%27_War" title="Thirty Years\' War">Thirty Years\' War</a>, defaulted on the government\'s bonds. Suddenly &#201;tienne Pascal\'s worth had dropped from nearly 66,000 livres to less than 7,300.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pascaline-CnAM_823-1-IMG_1506-black.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="4500" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Pascaline-CnAM_823-1-IMG_1506-black.jpg/220px-Pascaline-CnAM_823-1-IMG_1506-black.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Pascaline-CnAM_823-1-IMG_1506-black.jpg/330px-Pascaline-CnAM_823-1-IMG_1506-black.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/Pascaline-CnAM_823-1-IMG_1506-black.jpg/440px-Pascaline-CnAM_823-1-IMG_1506-black.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pascaline-CnAM_823-1-IMG_1506-black.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An early <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pascaline" title="Pascaline">Pascaline</a> on display at the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_des_Arts_et_M%C3%A9tiers" title="Mus&#233;e des Arts et M&#233;tiers">Mus&#233;e des Arts et M&#233;tiers</a>, Paris</div></div></div>\n<p>Like so many others, &#201;tienne was eventually forced to flee Paris because of his opposition to the fiscal policies of <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_Richelieu" title="Cardinal Richelieu">Cardinal Richelieu</a>, leaving his three children in the care of his neighbour Madame Sainctot, a great beauty with an infamous past who kept one of the most glittering and intellectual salons in all France. It was only when Jacqueline performed well in a children\'s play with Richelieu in attendance that &#201;tienne was pardoned. In time, &#201;tienne was back in good graces with the cardinal and in 1639 had been appointed the king\'s commissioner of taxes in the city of <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a>&#8212;a city whose tax records, thanks to uprisings, were in utter chaos.\n</p><p>In 1642, in an effort to ease his father\'s endless, exhausting calculations, and recalculations, of taxes owed and paid (into which work the young Pascal had been recruited), Pascal, not yet 19, constructed a mechanical calculator capable of addition and subtraction, called <i><a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_calculator" title="Pascal\'s calculator">Pascal\'s calculator</a></i> or the <i>Pascaline</i>. Of the eight Pascalines known to have survived, four are held by the <a href="/wiki/Mus%C3%A9e_des_Arts_et_M%C3%A9tiers" title="Mus&#233;e des Arts et M&#233;tiers">Mus&#233;e des Arts et M&#233;tiers</a> in Paris and one more by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zwinger_museum" title="Zwinger museum">Zwinger museum</a> in <a href="/wiki/Dresden" title="Dresden">Dresden</a>, Germany, exhibit two of his original mechanical calculators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Although these machines are pioneering forerunners to a further 400 years of development of mechanical methods of calculation, and in a sense to the later field of <a href="/wiki/Computer_engineering" title="Computer engineering">computer engineering</a>, the calculator failed to be a great commercial success. Partly because it was still quite cumbersome to use in practice, but probably primarily because it was extraordinarily expensive, the Pascaline became little more than a toy, and a <a href="/wiki/Status_symbol" title="Status symbol">status symbol</a>, for the very rich both in France and elsewhere in Europe. Pascal continued to make improvements to his design through the next decade, and he refers to some 50 machines that were built to his design.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions_to_mathematics">Contributions to mathematics</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:PascalTriangleAnimated2.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="260" decoding="async" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/PascalTriangleAnimated2.gif/220px-PascalTriangleAnimated2.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0d/PascalTriangleAnimated2.gif 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:PascalTriangleAnimated2.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_triangle" title="Pascal\'s triangle">Pascal\'s triangle</a>. Each number is the sum of the two directly above it. The triangle demonstrates many mathematical properties in addition to showing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Binomial_coefficients" title="Binomial coefficients">binomial coefficients</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Pascal continued to influence mathematics throughout his life. His <i>Trait&#233; du triangle arithm&#233;tique</i> ("Treatise on the Arithmetical Triangle") of 1653 described a convenient tabular presentation for <a href="/wiki/Binomial_coefficient" title="Binomial coefficient">binomial coefficients</a>, now called <a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_triangle" title="Pascal\'s triangle">Pascal\'s triangle</a>. The triangle can also be represented:\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th style="width:20px;">\n</th>\n<th style="width:20px;">0\n</th>\n<th style="width:20px;">1\n</th>\n<th style="width:20px;">2\n</th>\n<th style="width:20px;">3\n</th>\n<th style="width:20px;">4\n</th>\n<th style="width:20px;">5\n</th>\n<th style="width:20px;">6\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>0</b></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>1\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>1</b></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>2</td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td>4</td>\n<td>5</td>\n<td>6</td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>2</b></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>3</td>\n<td>6</td>\n<td>10</td>\n<td>15</td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>3</b></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>4</td>\n<td>10</td>\n<td>20</td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>4</b></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>5</td>\n<td>15</td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>5</b></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td>6</td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>6</b></td>\n<td>1</td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td></td>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table><p>He defines the numbers in the triangle by <a href="/wiki/Recursion" title="Recursion">recursion</a>: Call the number in the (<i>m</i>&#160;+&#160;1)th row and (<i>n</i>&#160;+&#160;1)th column <i>t</i><sub><i>mn</i></sub>. Then <i>t</i><sub><i>mn</i></sub>&#160;=&#160;<i>t</i><sub><i>m</i>&#8211;1,<i>n</i></sub>&#160;+&#160;<i>t</i><sub><i>m</i>,<i>n</i>&#8211;1</sub>, for <i>m</i>&#160;=&#160;0,&#160;1,&#160;2,&#160;... and <i>n</i>&#160;=&#160;0,&#160;1,&#160;2,&#160;...  The boundary conditions are <i>t</i><sub><i>m</i>,&#8722;1</sub>&#160;=&#160;0, <i>t</i><sub>&#8722;1,<i>n</i></sub>&#160;=&#160;0 for <i>m</i>&#160;=&#160;1,&#160;2,&#160;3,&#160;... and <i>n</i>&#160;=&#160;1,&#160;2,&#160;3,&#160;...  The generator <i>t</i><sub>00</sub>&#160;=&#160;1.  Pascal concludes with the proof,\n</p><dl><dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math alttext="{\\displaystyle t_{mn}={\\frac {(m+n)(m+n-1)\\cdots (m+1)}{n(n-1)\\cdots 1}}.}" xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<msub>\n<mi>t</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mi>n</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mfrac>\n<mrow>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>&#8943;<!-- &#8943; --></mo>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>m</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mrow>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mo>&#8722;<!-- &#8722; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>&#8943;<!-- &#8943; --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</mfrac>\n</mrow>\n<mo>.</mo>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle t_{mn}={\\frac {(m+n)(m+n-1)\\cdots (m+1)}{n(n-1)\\cdots 1}}.}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="{\\displaystyle t_{mn}={\\frac {(m+n)(m+n-1)\\cdots (m+1)}{n(n-1)\\cdots 1}}.}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/3cf8af8b1e4c33e7686fdb868864fe2b914598bf" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:39.605ex; height:6.509ex;"/></span></dd></dl>\n<p>In 1654, he proved <a href="/wiki/Faulhaber%27s_formula" title="Faulhaber\'s formula"><i>Pascal\'s identity</i></a> relating the sums of the <i>p</i>-th powers of the first <i>n</i> positive integers for <i>p</i> = 0, 1, 2, ..., <i>k</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1654, prompted by his friend the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chevalier_de_M%C3%A9r%C3%A9" title="Chevalier de M&#233;r&#233;">Chevalier de M&#233;r&#233;</a>, he corresponded with <a href="/wiki/Pierre_de_Fermat" title="Pierre de Fermat">Pierre de Fermat</a> on the subject of gambling problems, and from that collaboration was born the mathematical theory of <a href="/wiki/Probability" title="Probability">probabilities</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDevlin24_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDevlin24-15">[15]</a></sup> The specific problem was that of two players who want to finish a game early and, given the current circumstances of the game, want to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Division_of_the_stakes" title="Division of the stakes">divide the stakes fairly</a>, based on the chance each has of winning the game from that point. From this discussion, the notion of <a href="/wiki/Expected_value" title="Expected value">expected value</a> was introduced. Pascal later (in the <i>Pens&#233;es</i>) used a probabilistic argument, <a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_Wager" title="Pascal\'s Wager">Pascal\'s Wager</a>, to justify belief in God and a virtuous life. The work done by Fermat and Pascal into the calculus of probabilities laid important groundwork for <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz" title="Gottfried Wilhelm Leibniz">Leibniz</a>\' formulation of the <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">calculus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After a religious experience in 1654, Pascal mostly gave up work in mathematics.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy_of_mathematics">Philosophy of mathematics</span></h3>\n<p>Pascal\'s major contribution to the <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_mathematics" title="Philosophy of mathematics">philosophy of mathematics</a> came with his <i>De l\'Esprit g&#233;om&#233;trique</i> ("Of the Geometrical Spirit"), originally written as a preface to a geometry textbook for one of the famous "<i><a href="/wiki/Petites_%C3%A9coles_de_Port-Royal" title="Petites &#233;coles de Port-Royal">Petites-Ecoles de Port-Royal</a>" ("Little Schools of Port-Royal")</i>. The work was unpublished until over a century after his death. Here, Pascal looked into the issue of discovering truths, arguing that the ideal of such a method would be to found all propositions on already established truths. At the same time, however, he claimed this was impossible because such established truths would require other truths to back them up&#8212;first principles, therefore, cannot be reached. Based on this, Pascal argued that the procedure used in geometry was as perfect as possible, with certain principles assumed and other propositions developed from them. Nevertheless, there was no way to know the assumed principles to be true.\n</p><p>Pascal also used <i>De l\'Esprit g&#233;om&#233;trique</i> to develop a theory of <a href="/wiki/Definition" title="Definition">definition</a>. He distinguished between definitions which are conventional labels defined by the writer and definitions which are within the language and understood by everyone because they naturally designate their referent. The second type would be characteristic of the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Essentialism" title="Essentialism">essentialism</a>. Pascal claimed that only definitions of the first type were important to science and mathematics, arguing that those fields should adopt the philosophy of <a href="/wiki/Formalism_(philosophy)" title="Formalism (philosophy)">formalism</a> as formulated by Descartes.\n</p><p>In <i>De l\'Art de persuader</i> ("On the Art of Persuasion"), Pascal looked deeper into geometry\'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axiomatic_method" title="Axiomatic method">axiomatic method</a>, specifically the question of how people come to be convinced of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Axioms" title="Axioms">axioms</a> upon which later conclusions are based. Pascal agreed with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montaigne" title="Montaigne">Montaigne</a> that achieving certainty in these axioms and conclusions through human methods is impossible. He asserted that these principles can be grasped only through intuition, and that this fact underscored the necessity for submission to God in searching out truths.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Contributions_to_the_physical_sciences">Contributions to the physical sciences</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pascal%27s_Barrel.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="723" data-file-width="401" decoding="async" height="397" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pascal%27s_Barrel.png/220px-Pascal%27s_Barrel.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Pascal%27s_Barrel.png/330px-Pascal%27s_Barrel.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/51/Pascal%27s_Barrel.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pascal%27s_Barrel.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An illustration of the (apocryphal) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_barrel" title="Pascal\'s barrel">Pascal\'s barrel</a> experiment</div></div></div>\n<p>Pascal\'s work in the fields of the study of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hydrodynamics" title="Hydrodynamics">hydrodynamics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hydrostatics" title="Hydrostatics">hydrostatics</a> centered on the principles of <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fluid" title="Hydraulic fluid">hydraulic fluids</a>. His inventions include the <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_press" title="Hydraulic press">hydraulic press</a> (using hydraulic pressure to multiply force) and the <a href="/wiki/Syringe" title="Syringe">syringe</a>. He proved that hydrostatic pressure depends not on the weight of the fluid but on the elevation difference. He demonstrated this principle by attaching a thin tube to a barrel full of water and filling the tube with water up to the level of the third floor of a building. This caused the barrel to leak, in what became known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_barrel" title="Pascal\'s barrel">Pascal\'s barrel</a> experiment.\n</p><p>By 1647, Pascal had learned of <a href="/wiki/Evangelista_Torricelli" title="Evangelista Torricelli">Evangelista Torricelli</a>\'s experimentation with <a href="/wiki/Barometer" title="Barometer">barometers</a>. Having replicated an experiment that involved placing a tube filled with mercury upside down in a bowl of mercury, Pascal questioned what force kept some mercury in the tube and what filled the space above the mercury in the tube. At the time, most scientists contended that, rather than a <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">vacuum</a>, some invisible matter was present. This was based on the Aristotelian notion that creation was a thing of substance, whether visible or invisible; and that this substance was forever in motion. Furthermore, "Everything that is in motion must be moved by something," <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a> declared.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Therefore, to the Aristotelian trained scientists of Pascal\'s time, a vacuum was an impossibility. How so? As proof it was pointed out:\n</p>\n<ul><li>Light passed through the so-called "vacuum" in the glass tube.</li>\n<li>Aristotle wrote how everything moved, and must be moved by something.</li>\n<li>Therefore, since there had to be an invisible "something" to move the light through the glass tube, there was no vacuum in the tube. Not in the glass tube or anywhere else. Vacuums &#8211; the absence of any and everything &#8211; were simply an impossibility.</li></ul>\n<p>Following more experimentation in this vein, in 1647 Pascal produced <i>Experiences nouvelles touchant le vide</i> ("New experiments with the vacuum"), which detailed basic rules describing to what degree various liquids could be supported by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_pressure" title="Air pressure">air pressure</a>. It also provided reasons why it was indeed a vacuum above the column of liquid in a barometer tube. This work was followed by <i>R&#233;cit de la grande exp&#233;rience de l\'&#233;quilibre des liqueurs</i> ("Account of the great experiment on equilibrium in liquids") published in 1648.\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torricellian_vacuum" title="Torricellian vacuum">Torricellian vacuum</a> found that air pressure is equal to the weight of 30 inches of mercury. If air has a finite weight, Earth\'s atmosphere must have a maximum height. Pascal reasoned that if true, air pressure on a high mountain must be less than at a lower altitude. He lived near the <a href="/wiki/Puy_de_D%C3%B4me" title="Puy de D&#244;me">Puy de D&#244;me</a> mountain, 4,790 feet (1,460&#160;m) tall, but his health was poor so could not climb it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ley196606_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-ley196606-18">[18]</a></sup> On 19 September 1648, after many months of Pascal\'s friendly but insistent prodding, <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Florin_P%C3%A9rier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Florin P&#233;rier (page does not exist)">Florin P&#233;rier</a>, husband of Pascal\'s elder sister Gilberte, was finally able to carry out the fact-finding mission vital to Pascal\'s theory. The account, written by P&#233;rier, reads:\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303468">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>The weather was chancy last Saturday...[but] around five o\'clock that morning...the Puy-de-D&#244;me was visible...so I decided to give it a try. Several important people of the city of <a href="/wiki/Clermont-Ferrand" title="Clermont-Ferrand">Clermont</a> had asked me to let them know when I would make the ascent...I was delighted to have them with me in this great work...\n</p><p>...at eight o\'clock we met in the gardens of the Minim Fathers, which has the lowest elevation in town....First I poured 16 pounds of <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">quicksilver</a>...into a vessel...then took several glass tubes...each four feet long and <a href="/wiki/Hermetic_seal" title="Hermetic seal">hermetically sealed</a> at one end and opened at the other...then placed them in the vessel [of quicksilver]...I found the quick silver stood at 26" and 3&#8203;<span class="frac nowrap"><sup>1</sup>&#8260;<sub>2</sub></span> lines above the quicksilver in the vessel...I repeated the experiment two more times while standing in the same spot...[they] produced the same result each time...\n</p><p>I attached one of the tubes to the vessel and marked the height of the quicksilver and...asked Father Chastin, one of the Minim Brothers...to watch if any changes should occur through the day...Taking the other tube and a portion of the quick silver...I walked to the top of Puy-de-D&#244;me, about 500 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fathoms" title="Fathoms">fathoms</a> higher than the monastery, where upon experiment...found that the quicksilver reached a height of only 23" and 2 lines...I repeated the experiment five times with care...each at different points on the summit...found the same height of quicksilver...in each case...<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup>\n</p>\n</blockquote>\n<p>Pascal replicated the experiment in Paris by carrying a barometer up to the top of the bell tower at the church of <a href="/wiki/Saint-Jacques_Tower" title="Saint-Jacques Tower">Saint-Jacques-de-la-Boucherie</a>, a height of about 50 metres. The mercury dropped two lines.\n</p><p>In the face of criticism that some invisible matter must exist in Pascal\'s empty space, Pascal, in his reply to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Estienne_Noel&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Estienne Noel (page does not exist)">Estienne Noel</a>, gave one of the 17th century\'s major statements on the scientific method, which is a striking anticipation of the idea popularised by <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a> that scientific theories are characterised by their <a href="/wiki/Falsifiability" title="Falsifiability">falsifiability</a>: "In order to show that a hypothesis is evident, it does not suffice that all the phenomena follow from it; instead, if it leads to something contrary to a single one of the phenomena, that suffices to establish its falsity."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> His insistence on the existence of the vacuum also led to conflict with other prominent scientists, including Descartes.\n</p><p>Pascal introduced a primitive form of <a href="/wiki/Roulette" title="Roulette">roulette</a> and the roulette wheel in his search for a <a href="/wiki/Perpetual_motion" title="Perpetual motion">perpetual motion</a> machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span id="Adult_life.2C_religion.2C_philosophy.2C_and_literature"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Adult_life,_religion,_philosophy,_and_literature">Adult life, religion, philosophy, and literature</span></h2>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Catholic_philosophy" title="Template talk:Catholic philosophy"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Catholic_philosophy&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>For after all what is man in nature? A nothing in relation to infinity, all in relation to nothing, a central point between nothing and all and infinitely far from understanding either. The ends of things and their beginnings are impregnably concealed from him in an impenetrable secret. He is equally incapable of seeing the nothingness out of which he was drawn and the infinite in which he is engulfed.\n</p></blockquote>\n<dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd><dl><dd>Blaise Pascal, <i>Pens&#233;es</i> No. 72</dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl></dd></dl>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_conversion">Religious conversion</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pascal_Pajou_Louvre_RF2981.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3008" data-file-width="2000" decoding="async" height="256" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pascal_Pajou_Louvre_RF2981.jpg/170px-Pascal_Pajou_Louvre_RF2981.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pascal_Pajou_Louvre_RF2981.jpg/255px-Pascal_Pajou_Louvre_RF2981.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Pascal_Pajou_Louvre_RF2981.jpg/340px-Pascal_Pajou_Louvre_RF2981.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pascal_Pajou_Louvre_RF2981.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pascal studying the <a href="/wiki/Cycloid" title="Cycloid">cycloid</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Augustin_Pajou" title="Augustin Pajou">Augustin Pajou</a>, 1785, <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In the winter of 1646, Pascal\'s 58-year-old father broke his hip when he slipped and fell on an icy street of Rouen; given the man\'s age and the state of medicine in the 17th century, a <a href="/wiki/Hip_fracture" title="Hip fracture">broken hip</a> could be a very serious condition, perhaps even fatal. Rouen was home to two of the finest doctors in France: Monsieur Doctor Deslandes and Monsieur Doctor de La Bouteillerie. The elder Pascal "would not let anyone other than these men attend him...It was a good choice, for the old man survived and was able to walk again..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> But treatment and rehabilitation took three months, during which time La Bouteillerie and Deslandes had become regular visitors.\n</p><p>Both men were followers of <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Jean_Guillebert&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Jean Guillebert (page does not exist)">Jean Guillebert</a>, proponent of a splinter group from Catholic teaching known as <a href="/wiki/Jansenism" title="Jansenism">Jansenism</a>. This still fairly small sect was making surprising inroads into the French Catholic community at that time. It espoused rigorous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustinism" title="Augustinism">Augustinism</a>. Blaise spoke with the doctors frequently, and after their successful treatment of his father, borrowed from them works by Jansenist authors. In this period, Pascal experienced a sort of "first conversion" and began to write on theological subjects in the course of the following year.\n</p><p>Pascal fell away from this initial religious engagement and experienced a few years of what some biographers have called his "worldly period" (1648&#8211;54). His father died in 1651 and left his inheritance to Pascal and his sister Jacqueline, for whom Pascal acted as conservator. Jacqueline announced that she would soon become a <a href="/wiki/Postulant" title="Postulant">postulant</a> in the Jansenist convent of <a href="/wiki/Port-Royal-des-Champs" title="Port-Royal-des-Champs">Port-Royal</a>. Pascal was deeply affected and very sad, not because of her choice, but because of his chronic poor health; he needed her just as she had needed him.\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303468" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Suddenly there was war in the Pascal household. Blaise pleaded with Jacqueline not to leave, but she was adamant. He commanded her to stay, but that didn\'t work, either. At the heart of this was...Blaise\'s fear of abandonment...if Jacqueline entered Port-Royal, she would have to leave her inheritance behind...[but] nothing would change her mind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p></blockquote>\n<p>By the end of October in 1651, a truce had been reached between brother and sister. In return for a healthy annual stipend, Jacqueline signed over her part of the inheritance to her brother.  Gilberte had already been given her inheritance in the form of a dowry. In early January, Jacqueline left for Port-Royal. On that day, according to Gilberte concerning her brother, "He retired very sadly to his rooms without seeing Jacqueline, who was waiting in the little parlor..."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>\nIn early June 1653, after what must have seemed like endless badgering from Jacqueline,\nPascal formally signed over the whole of his sister\'s inheritance to Port-Royal, which, to him, "had begun to smell like a cult."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> With two thirds of his father\'s estate now gone, the 29-year-old Pascal was now consigned to genteel poverty.\n</p><p>For a while, Pascal pursued the life of a bachelor. During visits to his sister at Port-Royal in 1654, he displayed contempt for affairs of the world but was not drawn to God.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ep52_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-ep52-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Brush_with_death">Brush with death</span></h3>\n<p>On 23 November 1654, between 10:30 and 12:30 at night, Pascal had an intense <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vision_(religion)" title="Vision (religion)">religious vision</a> and immediately recorded the experience in a brief note to himself which began: "Fire. God of Abraham, God of Isaac, God of Jacob, not of the philosophers and the scholars..." and concluded by quoting Psalm 119:16: "I will not forget thy word. Amen." He seems to have carefully sewn this document into his coat and always transferred it when he changed clothes; a servant discovered it only by chance after his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-oc618_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-oc618-27">[27]</a></sup> This piece is now known as the <i>Memorial</i>. The story of the carriage accident<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (July 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> as having led to the experience described in the <i>Memorial</i> is disputed by some scholars.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>\nHis belief and religious commitment revitalized, Pascal visited the older of two convents at <a href="/wiki/Port-Royal-des-Champs" title="Port-Royal-des-Champs">Port-Royal</a> for a two-week retreat in January 1655. For the next four years, he regularly travelled between Port-Royal and Paris. It was at this point immediately after his conversion when he began writing his first major literary work on religion, the <i>Provincial Letters</i>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Provincial_Letters">The <i>Provincial Letters</i></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Lettres_provinciales" title="Lettres provinciales">Lettres provinciales</a></div>\n<table class="vertical-navbox nowraplinks hlist" style="float:right;clear:right;width:22.0em;margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em;background:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;padding:0.2em;border-spacing:0.4em 0;text-align:center;line-height:1.4em;font-size:88%"><tbody><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;background:#ddd;padding:0.2em 0.4em;font-size:110%;">\n<a href="/wiki/French_literature" title="French literature">French literature</a></th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding:0.1em 0.4em 0.7em;font-size:100%;">\n<a href="/wiki/Category:French_literature" title="Category:French literature">by category</a></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;background:#ddd;padding:0.2em 0.4em;font-size:110%;">\nFrench literary history</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding:0.1em 0.4em 0.7em;font-size:100%;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Medieval_French_literature" title="Medieval French literature">Medieval</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/French_Renaissance_literature" title="French Renaissance literature">Renaissance</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/17th-century_French_literature" href="/wiki/19th-century_French_literature" title="19th-century French literature">19th</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/20th-century_French_literature" title="20th-century French literature">20th century</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Contemporary_French_literature" title="Contemporary French literature">Contemporary</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;background:#ddd;padding:0.2em 0.4em;font-size:110%;">\nFrench writers</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding:0.1em 0.4em 0.7em;font-size:100%;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_French-language_authors" title="List of French-language authors">Chronological list</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:French_writers" title="Category:French writers">Writers by category</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:French_essayists" title="Category:French essayists">Essayists</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:French_novelists" title="Category:French novelists">Novelists</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:French_dramatists_and_playwrights" title="Category:French dramatists and playwrights">Playwrights</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:French_poets" title="Category:French poets">Poets</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:French_short_story_writers" title="Category:French short story writers">Short story writers</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Category:French_children%27s_writers" title="Category:French children\'s writers">Children\'s writers</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><th style="padding:0.1em;background:#ddd;padding:0.2em 0.4em;font-size:110%;">\nPortals</th></tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em;padding:0.1em 0.4em 0.7em;font-size:100%;">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Portal:France" title="Portal:France">France</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Portal:French_language_and_French-speaking_world" title="Portal:French language and French-speaking world">French language</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Portal:Literature" title="Portal:Literature">Literature</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Portal:French_and_Francophone_literature" title="Portal:French and Francophone literature">French/Francophone literature</a></li></ul></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:French_literature_sidebar" title="Template:French literature sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:French_literature_sidebar" title="Template talk:French literature sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:French_literature_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Beginning in 1656&#8211;57, Pascal published his memorable attack on <a href="/wiki/Casuistry" title="Casuistry">casuistry</a>, a popular <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethical</a> method used by <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a> thinkers in the early modern period (especially the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuits" title="Jesuits">Jesuits</a>, and in particular <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Escobar_y_Mendoza" title="Antonio Escobar y Mendoza">Antonio Escobar</a>). Pascal denounced casuistry as the mere use of complex reasoning to justify moral laxity and all sorts of <a href="/wiki/Sin" title="Sin">sins</a>. The 18-letter series was published between 1656 and 1657 under the pseudonym Louis de Montalte and incensed <a href="/wiki/Louis_XIV_of_France" title="Louis XIV of France">Louis XIV</a>. The king ordered that the book be <a href="/wiki/Book_burning" title="Book burning">shredded and burnt</a> in 1660. In 1661, in the midsts of the <a href="/wiki/Formulary_controversy" title="Formulary controversy">formulary controversy</a>, the Jansenist school at Port-Royal was condemned and closed down; those involved with the school had to sign a 1656 <a href="/wiki/Papal_bull" title="Papal bull">papal bull</a> condemning the teachings of Jansen as heretical. The final letter from Pascal, in 1657, had defied <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexander_VII" title="Alexander VII">Alexander VII</a> himself. Even Pope Alexander, while publicly opposing them, nonetheless was persuaded by Pascal\'s arguments.\n</p><p>Aside from their religious influence, the <i>Provincial Letters</i> were popular as a literary work. Pascal\'s use of humor, mockery, and vicious satire in his arguments made the letters ripe for public consumption, and influenced the prose of later French writers like <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean-Jacques_Rousseau" title="Jean-Jacques Rousseau">Jean-Jacques Rousseau</a>.\n</p><p>Charles Perrault wrote of the <i>Letters</i>: "Everything is there&#8212;purity of language, nobility of thought, solidity in reasoning, finesse in raillery, and throughout an <i>agr&#233;ment</i> not to be found anywhere else."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="The_Pens.C3.A9es"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Pens&#233;es">The <i>Pens&#233;es</i></span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Pens%C3%A9es" title="Pens&#233;es">Pens&#233;es</a></div>\n<p>Pascal\'s most influential theological work, referred to posthumously as the <i>Pens&#233;es</i> ("Thoughts"), was not completed before his death. It was to have been a sustained and coherent examination and defense of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christian_faith" title="Christian faith">Christian faith</a>, with the original title <i>Apologie de la religion Chr&#233;tienne</i> ("Defense of the Christian Religion"). The first version of the numerous scraps of paper found after his death appeared in print as a book in 1669 titled <i>Pens&#233;es de M. Pascal sur la religion, et sur quelques autres sujets</i> ("Thoughts of M. Pascal on religion, and on some other subjects") and soon thereafter became a classic. One of the <i>Apologie</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">\'</span>s main strategies was to use the contradictory philosophies of <a href="/wiki/Skepticism" title="Skepticism">skepticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stoicism" title="Stoicism">stoicism</a>, personalized by <a href="/wiki/Michel_de_Montaigne" title="Michel de Montaigne">Montaigne</a> on one hand, and <a href="/wiki/Epictetus" title="Epictetus">Epictetus</a> on the other, in order to bring the unbeliever to such despair and confusion that he would embrace God.\n</p><p>Pascal\'s <i>Pens&#233;es</i> is widely considered to be a masterpiece, and a landmark in <i>French prose</i>. When commenting on one particular section (Thought #72), <a href="/wiki/Charles_Augustin_Sainte-Beuve" title="Charles Augustin Sainte-Beuve">Sainte-Beuve</a> praised it as the finest pages in the <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French language</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-finest_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-finest-30">[30]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Will_Durant" title="Will Durant">Will Durant</a> hailed the Pens&#233;es as "the most eloquent book in French prose".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eloquent_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-eloquent-31">[31]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Last_works_and_death">Last works and death</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Epitaph_Blaise_Pascal_Saint-Etienne.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1628" data-file-width="1144" decoding="async" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Epitaph_Blaise_Pascal_Saint-Etienne.jpg/220px-Epitaph_Blaise_Pascal_Saint-Etienne.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Epitaph_Blaise_Pascal_Saint-Etienne.jpg/330px-Epitaph_Blaise_Pascal_Saint-Etienne.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Epitaph_Blaise_Pascal_Saint-Etienne.jpg/440px-Epitaph_Blaise_Pascal_Saint-Etienne.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Epitaph_Blaise_Pascal_Saint-Etienne.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Pascal\'s epitaph in <a href="/wiki/Saint-%C3%89tienne-du-Mont" title="Saint-&#201;tienne-du-Mont">Saint-&#201;tienne-du-Mont</a>, where he was buried</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/T._S._Eliot" title="T. S. Eliot">T. S. Eliot</a> described him during this phase of his life as "a man of the world among ascetics, and an ascetic among men of the world." Pascal\'s ascetic lifestyle derived from a belief that it was natural and necessary for a person to suffer. In 1659, Pascal fell seriously ill. During his last years, he frequently tried to reject the ministrations of his doctors, saying, "Sickness is the natural state of Christians."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m104-_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-m104--32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Louis XIV suppressed the Jansenist movement at Port-Royal in 1661. In response, Pascal wrote one of his final works, <i>&#201;crit sur la signature du formulaire</i> ("Writ on the Signing of the Form"), exhorting the Jansenists not to give in. Later that year, his sister Jacqueline died, which convinced Pascal to cease his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polemics" title="Polemics">polemics</a> on <a href="/wiki/Jansenism" title="Jansenism">Jansenism</a>. Pascal\'s last major achievement, returning to his mechanical genius, was inaugurating perhaps the first bus line, the <a href="/wiki/Carrosses_%C3%A0_cinq_sols" title="Carrosses &#224; cinq sols">carrosses &#224; cinq sols</a>, moving passengers within Paris in a carriage with many seats.\n</p><p>In 1662, Pascal\'s illness became more violent, and his emotional condition had severely worsened since his sister\'s death. Aware that his health was fading quickly, he sought a move to the hospital for incurable diseases, but his doctors declared that he was too unstable to be carried. In Paris on 18 August 1662, Pascal went into convulsions and received <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extreme_unction" title="Extreme unction">extreme unction</a>. He died the next morning, his last words being "May God never abandon me," and was buried in the cemetery of <a href="/wiki/Saint-%C3%89tienne-du-Mont" title="Saint-&#201;tienne-du-Mont">Saint-&#201;tienne-du-Mont</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m104-_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-m104--32">[32]</a></sup>\n</p><p>An <a href="/wiki/Autopsy" title="Autopsy">autopsy</a> performed after his death revealed grave problems with his stomach and other organs of his abdomen, along with <a href="/wiki/Brain_damage" title="Brain damage">damage to his brain</a>. Despite the autopsy, the cause of his poor health was never precisely determined, though speculation focuses on <a href="/wiki/Tuberculosis" title="Tuberculosis">tuberculosis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stomach_cancer" title="Stomach cancer">stomach cancer</a>, or a combination of the two.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-m103_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-m103-33">[33]</a></sup> The headaches which afflicted Pascal are generally attributed to his brain <a href="/wiki/Lesion" title="Lesion">lesion</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:001Paskal.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="347" data-file-width="336" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/001Paskal.JPG/220px-001Paskal.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/001Paskal.JPG/330px-001Paskal.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f9/001Paskal.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:001Paskal.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Death_mask" title="Death mask">Death mask</a> of Blaise Pascal.</div></div></div>\n<p>In honour of his scientific contributions, the name <i>Pascal</i> has been given to the <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(unit)" title="Pascal (unit)">SI unit of pressure</a>, to a <a href="/wiki/Pascal_(programming_language)" title="Pascal (programming language)">programming language</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_law" title="Pascal\'s law">Pascal\'s law</a> (an important principle of hydrostatics), and as mentioned above, Pascal\'s triangle and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pascal%27s_wager" title="Pascal\'s wager">Pascal\'s wager</a> still bear his name.\n</p><p>Pascal\'s development of probability theory was his most influential contribution to mathematics. Originally applied to gambling, today it is extremely important in economics, especially in <a href="/wiki/Actuarial_science" title="Actuarial science">actuarial science</a>. John Ross writes, "Probability theory and the discoveries following it changed the way we regard uncertainty, risk, decision-making, and an individual\'s and society\'s ability to influence the course of future events."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> However, it should be noted that Pascal and Fermat, though doing important early work in probability theory, did not develop the field very far. <a href="/wiki/Christiaan_Huygens" title="Christiaan Huygens">Christiaan Huygens</a>, learning of the subject from the correspondence of Pascal and Fermat, wrote the first book on the subject. Later figures who continued the development of the theory include <a href="/wiki/Abraham_de_Moivre" title="Abraham de Moivre">Abraham de Moivre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">Pierre-Simon Laplace</a>.\n</p><p>In literature, Pascal is regarded as one of the most important authors of the French Classical Period and is read today as one of the greatest masters of French prose. His use of satire and wit influenced later <a href="/wiki/Polemic" title="Polemic">polemicists</a>. The content of his literary work is best remembered for its strong opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Rationalism" title="Rationalism">rationalism</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes" title="Ren&#233; Descartes">Ren&#233; Descartes</a> and simultaneous assertion that the main countervailing philosophy, <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empiricism</a>, was also insufficient for determining major truths.\n</p><p>In France, prestigious annual awards, <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal_Chair" title="Blaise Pascal Chair">Blaise Pascal Chairs</a> are given to outstanding international scientists to conduct their research in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%8Ele-de-France_(region)" title="&#206;le-de-France (region)">Ile de France</a> region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> One of the Universities of <a href="/wiki/Clermont-Ferrand" title="Clermont-Ferrand">Clermont-Ferrand</a>, France &#8211; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Universit%C3%A9_Blaise_Pascal" title="Universit&#233; Blaise Pascal">Universit&#233; Blaise Pascal</a> &#8211; is named after him. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Waterloo" title="University of Waterloo">University of Waterloo</a>, Ontario, Canada, holds an annual math contest named in his honour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Pascalian theology has grown out of his perspective that we are, according to Wood, "born into a duplicitous world that shapes us into duplicitous subjects and so we find it easy to reject God continually and deceive ourselves about our own sinfulness".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Roberto_Rossellini" title="Roberto Rossellini">Roberto Rossellini</a> directed a filmed biopic, <i>Blaise Pascal</i>, which originally aired on Italian television in 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> Pascal was a subject of the first edition of the 1984 <a href="/wiki/BBC_Two" title="BBC Two">BBC Two</a> documentary, <i><a href="/wiki/Sea_of_Faith_(TV_series)" title="Sea of Faith (TV series)">Sea of Faith</a></i>, presented by <a href="/wiki/Don_Cupitt" title="Don Cupitt">Don Cupitt</a>.\n</p><p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Nvidia" title="Nvidia">Nvidia</a> announced its new <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pascal_microarchitecture" title="Pascal microarchitecture">Pascal microarchitecture</a>, which is named for Pascal. The first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Graphics_cards" title="Graphics cards">graphics cards</a> featuring Pascal were released in 2016.\n</p><p>The 2017 game <i><a href="/wiki/Nier:_Automata" title="Nier: Automata">Nier: Automata</a></i> has multiple characters named after famous philosophers; one of these is a sentient pacifistic machine named Pascal, who serves as a major supporting character. Pascal creates a village for machines to live peacefully with the androids they\'re at war with and acts as a parental figure for other machines trying to adapt to their newly-found individuality.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Works">Works</span></h2>\n<ul><li><i>Essai pour les coniques</i> [Essay on conics] (1639)</li>\n<li><i>Experiences nouvelles touchant le vide</i> [New experiments with the vacuum] (1647)</li>\n<li><i>R&#233;cit de la grande exp&#233;rience de l\'&#233;quilibre des liqueurs</i> [Account of the great experiment on equilibrium in liquids] (1648)</li>\n<li><i>Trait&#233; du triangle arithm&#233;tique</i> [Treatise on the arithmetic triangle] (1653)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Lettres_provinciales" title="Lettres provinciales">Lettres provinciales</a></i> [The provincial letters] (1656&#8211;57)</li>\n<li><i>De l\'Esprit g&#233;om&#233;trique</i> [On the geometrical spirit] (1657 or 1658)</li>\n<li><i>&#201;crit sur la signature du formulaire</i> (1661)</li>\n<li><i>Trait&#233; du triangle arithm&#233;tique</i> (Treatise on arithmetical triangle) (written c. 1654;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> publ. 1665)</li>\n<li><i><a href="/wiki/Pens%C3%A9es" title="Pens&#233;es">Pens&#233;es</a></i> [Thoughts] (incomplete at death; publ. 1670)</li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>\n<ul><li><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Portal-puzzle.svg"><img alt="Portal-puzzle.svg" class="noviewer" data-file-height="28" data-file-width="32" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/16px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/24px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/fd/Portal-puzzle.svg/32px-Portal-puzzle.svg.png 2x" width="16"/></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Blaise_Pascal" title="Portal:Blaise Pascal">Blaise Pascal portal</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scientific_revolution" title="Scientific revolution">Scientific revolution</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_pioneers_in_computer_science" title="List of pioneers in computer science">List of pioneers in computer science</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_works_by_Eug%C3%A8ne_Guillaume" title="List of works by Eug&#232;ne Guillaume">List of works by Eug&#232;ne Guillaume</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; J. Brill, 2005), 126 n.7</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">\n<a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology" title="Massachusetts Institute of Technology">MIT</a>, <a class="external text" href="http://web.mit.edu/invent/iow/pascal.html" rel="nofollow">"Inventor of the Week Archive: Pascal&#160;: Mechanical Calculator"</a>, May 2003. "Pascal worked on many versions of the devices, leading to his attempt to create a perpetual motion machine.
